<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Glioblastoma</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<tr class="noprint">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q282142" title="d:Q282142">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Glioblastoma</b>, also known as <b>glioblastoma multiforme</b> (<b>GBM</b>), is the most aggressive <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> that begins within the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2012_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2012-1">[1]</a></sup> Signs and symptoms of glioblastoma are initially non-specific. They may include <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headaches</a>, personality changes, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, and symptoms similar to those of a <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Worsening of symptoms is often rapid. This can progress to <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">unconsciousness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cause of most cases is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup> Uncommon risk factors include <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic disorders</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Neurofibromatosis" title="Neurofibromatosis">neurofibromatosis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Fraumeni_syndrome" title="Li Fraumeni syndrome">Li Fraumeni syndrome</a> and previous <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> Glioblastomas represent 15% of <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">brain tumors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young2015-2">[2]</a></sup> They can either start from normal brain cells or develop from an already existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-grade_astrocytoma" title="Low-grade astrocytoma">low-grade astrocytoma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C3.8_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C3.8-5">[5]</a></sup> The diagnosis is typically made by a combination of <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI_scan" title="MRI scan">MRI scan</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tissue_biopsy" title="Tissue biopsy">tissue biopsy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Young2015-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is no clear way to prevent the disease. Typically treatment involves <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> after which <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> and radiation therapy are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> The medication <a href="/wiki/Temozolomide" title="Temozolomide">temozolomide</a> is frequently used as part of chemotherapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTG1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> High dose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corticosteroids" title="Corticosteroids">steroids</a> may be used to help reduce swelling and decrease symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young2015_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Young2015-2">[2]</a></sup> It is unclear whether trying to remove all or simply most of the cancer is better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite maximum treatment, the cancer usually recurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> The most common length of survival following diagnosis is 12 to 15 months with less than 3% to 5% of people surviving longer than five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> Without treatment survival is typically 3 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It is the most common cancer that begins within the brain and the second most common brain tumor after <a href="/wiki/Meningioma" title="Meningioma">meningioma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2012_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2012-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mc2016_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mc2016-10">[10]</a></sup> About 3 per 100,000 people develop the disease a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> It most often begins around 64 years of age and occurs more commonly in males than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</a> is being studied in glioblastoma with promising results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although common symptoms of the disease include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_loss" title="Memory loss">memory loss</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hemiparesis" title="Hemiparesis">hemiparesis</a>, the single most prevalent symptom is a progressive memory, personality, or neurological deficit due to <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal lobe</a> involvement. The kind of symptoms produced depends more on the location of the tumor than on its pathological properties. The tumor can start producing symptoms quickly, but occasionally is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asymptomatic_condition" title="Asymptomatic condition">asymptomatic condition</a> until it reaches an enormous size.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Risk factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For unknown reasons, GBM occurs more commonly in males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Most glioblastoma tumors appear to be sporadic, without any <a href="/wiki/Genetic_predisposition" title="Genetic predisposition">genetic predisposition</a>. No links have been found between glioblastoma and <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">consumption of cured meat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_fields" title="Electromagnetic fields">electromagnetic fields</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Alcohol consumption may be a possible risk factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Glioblastoma has been associated with the viruses <a href="/wiki/SV40" title="SV40">SV40</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_herpesvirus_6" title="Human herpesvirus 6">HHV-6</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cytomegalovirus" title="Cytomegalovirus">cytomegalovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> There also appears to be a small link between <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> and glioblastoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> A 2006 analysis links brain cancer to lead exposure in the work-place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> There is an association of brain tumor incidence and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, suggesting that the <a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">anopheles</a> mosquito, the carrier of malaria, might transmit a virus or other agent that could cause glioblastoma<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> or that the immunosuppression associated with malaria could enhance viral replication. Also HHV-6 reactivates in response to hypersensitivity reactions from drugs and environmental chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other risk factors include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Sex: male (slightly more common in men than women)</li>
<li>Age: over 50 years old</li>
<li>Ethnicity: Caucasians, Hispanics, and Asians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Having a low-grade <a href="/wiki/Astrocytoma" title="Astrocytoma">astrocytoma</a> (brain tumor), which often, given enough time, develops into a higher-grade tumor</li>
<li>Having one of the following genetic disorders is associated with an increased incidence of gliomas:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neurofibromatosis" title="Neurofibromatosis">Neurofibromatosis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tuberous_sclerosis" title="Tuberous sclerosis">Tuberous sclerosis</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Von_Hippel-Lindau_disease" title="Von Hippel-Lindau disease">Von Hippel-Lindau disease</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li-Fraumeni_syndrome" title="Li-Fraumeni syndrome">Li-Fraumeni syndrome</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turcot_syndrome" title="Turcot syndrome">Turcot syndrome</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathogenesis">Pathogenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pathogenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Glioblastoma multiforme tumors are characterized by the presence of small areas of <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrotizing</a> <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> that are surrounded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaplastic" title="Anaplastic">anaplastic</a> cells. This characteristic, as well as the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperplastic" title="Hyperplastic">hyperplastic</a> blood vessels, differentiates the tumor from Grade 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrocytomas" title="Astrocytomas">astrocytomas</a>, which do not have these features.</p>
<p>GBMs usually form in the cerebral white matter, grow quickly, and can become very large before producing symptoms. Less than 10% form more slowly following degeneration of <a href="/wiki/Fibrillary_astrocytoma" title="Fibrillary astrocytoma">low-grade astrocytoma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anaplastic_astrocytoma" title="Anaplastic astrocytoma">anaplastic astrocytoma</a>. These are called secondary GBMs and are more common in younger patients (mean age 45 versus 62 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The tumor may extend into the meninges or <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_system" title="Ventricular system">ventricular</a> wall, leading to high protein content in the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a> (CSF) (&gt; 100 mg/dL), as well as an occasional <a href="/wiki/Pleocytosis" title="Pleocytosis">pleocytosis</a> of 10 to 100 cells, mostly <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malignant" title="Malignant">Malignant</a> cells carried in the CSF may spread (rarely) to the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> or cause meningeal gliomatosis. However, <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> of GBM beyond the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> is extremely unusual. About 50% of GBMs occupy more than one lobe of a hemisphere or are bilateral. Tumors of this type usually arise from the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">cerebrum</a> and may exhibit the classic infiltration across the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_callosum" title="Corpus callosum">corpus callosum</a>, producing a butterfly (bilateral) <a href="/wiki/Glioma" title="Glioma">glioma</a>.</p>
<p>The tumor may take on a variety of appearances, depending on the amount of hemorrhage, <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a>, or its age. A CT scan will usually show an inhomogeneous mass with a hypodense center and a variable <a href="/wiki/Ring-enhancing_lesion" title="Ring-enhancing lesion">ring of enhancement</a> surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a>. Mass effect from the tumor and edema may compress the ventricles and cause <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_alterations">Molecular alterations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Molecular alterations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Four subtypes of glioblastoma have been identified:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roel2010_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roel2010-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Classical : Ninety-seven percent of tumors in the 'classical' subtype carry extra copies of the <a href="/wiki/Epidermal_growth_factor_receptor" title="Epidermal growth factor receptor">epidermal growth factor receptor</a> (EGFR) gene, and most have higher than normal expression of <a href="/wiki/Epidermal_growth_factor_receptor" title="Epidermal growth factor receptor">epidermal growth factor receptor</a> (EGFR), whereas the gene <a href="/wiki/TP53" title="TP53">TP53</a> (p53), which is often mutated in glioblastoma, is rarely mutated in this subtype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Proneural subtype often has high rates of alterations in TP53 (p53), and in PDGFRA, the gene encoding a-type <a href="/wiki/Platelet-derived_growth_factor_receptor" title="Platelet-derived growth factor receptor">platelet-derived growth factor receptor</a>, and in IDH1, the gene encoding <a href="/wiki/Isocitrate_dehydrogenase" title="Isocitrate dehydrogenase">isocitrate dehydrogenase</a>-1.</li>
<li>The Mesenchymal subtype is characterized by high rates of mutations or other alterations in NF1, the gene encoding <a href="/wiki/Neurofibromin_1" title="Neurofibromin 1">Neurofibromin 1</a> and fewer alterations in the EGFR gene and less expression of EGFR than other types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Neural subtype was typified by the expression of neuron markers such as <a href="/wiki/NEFL" title="NEFL">NEFL</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamma-aminobutyric_acid_receptor_subunit_alpha-1" title="Gamma-aminobutyric acid receptor subunit alpha-1">GABRA1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptotagmin" title="Synaptotagmin">SYT1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chloride_potassium_symporter_5" title="Chloride potassium symporter 5">SLC12A5</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roel2010_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roel2010-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Many other genetic alterations have been described in glioblastoma, and the majority of them are clustered in two pathways, the <a href="/wiki/Retinoblastoma_protein" title="Retinoblastoma protein">RB</a> and the <a href="/wiki/PI3K/AKT/mTOR_pathway" title="PI3K/AKT/mTOR pathway">PI3K/AKT</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Glioblastomas have alterations in 68-78% and 88% of these pathways, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2012_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2012-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another important alteration is methylation of <a href="/wiki/O-6-methylguanine-DNA_methyltransferase" title="O-6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase">MGMT</a>, a "suicide" DNA repair enzyme. Methylation is described to impair DNA transcription and therefore, expression of the MGMT enzyme. Since an MGMT enzyme can only repair one DNA alkylation due to its suicide repair mechanism, reverse capacity is low and methylation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MGMT_promoter" title="MGMT promoter">MGMT gene promoter</a> greatly affects DNA-repair capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-36">[36]</a></sup> Indeed, MGMT methylation is associated with an improved response to treatment with DNA-damaging chemotherapeutics, such as temozolomide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glioblastoma_stem-like_cells">Glioblastoma stem-like cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Glioblastoma stem-like cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cancer cells with stem cell-like properties have been found in glioblastomas (this may be a cause of their resistance to conventional treatments, and high recurrence rate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> These so-called glioblastoma stem-like cells reside in a niche around arterioles, which protects these cells against therapy by maintaining a relatively hypoxic environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> A biomarker for cells in glioblastomas that exhibit <a href="/wiki/Cancer_stem_cell" title="Cancer stem cell">cancer stem cell</a> properties, the transcription factor <a href="/wiki/Hes3_signaling_axis" title="Hes3 signaling axis">Hes3</a>, has been shown to regulate their number when placed in culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>IDH1</i> gene encodes for the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Isocitrate_dehydrogenase" title="Isocitrate dehydrogenase">isocitrate dehydrogenase</a> 1 and is frequently mutated in glioblastoma (primary GBM: 5%, secondary GBM &gt;80%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-36">[36]</a></sup> By producing very high concentrations of the "oncometabolite" D-2-hydroxyglutarate and dysregulating the function of the wild-type IDH1-enzyme it induces profound changes to the metabolism of <i>IDH1</i>-mutated glioblastoma, compared with <i>IDH1</i> wild-type glioblastoma or healthy astrocytes. Among others, it increases the glioblastoma cells' dependence on <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> as an energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It has been hypothesized that <i>IDH1</i>-mutated glioblastoma are in a very high demand for glutamate and use this amino acid and neurotransmitter as a chemotactic signal. Since healthy astrocytes excrete glutamate, <i>IDH1</i>-mutated glioblastoma cells do not favor dense tumor structures but instead migrate, invade and disperse into healthy parts of the brain where glutamate concentrations are higher. This may explain the invasive behaviour of these <i>IDH1</i>-mutated glioblastoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ion_channels">Ion channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ion channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Furthermore, glioblastoma multiforme exhibits numerous alterations in genes that encode for ion channels, including upregulation of gBK potassium channels and ClC-3 chloride channels. It has been hypothesized that by upregulating these ion channels, glioblastoma tumor cells can facilitate increased ion movement over the cell membrane, thereby increasing H<sub>2</sub>O movement through osmosis, which aids glioblastoma cells in changing cellular volume very rapidly. This is helpful in their extremely aggressive invasive behavior, because quick adaptations in cellular volume can facilitate movement through the sinuous extracellular matrix of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrainCAConMark.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="895" data-file-width="698" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrainCAConMark.png/220px-BrainCAConMark.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrainCAConMark.png/330px-BrainCAConMark.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BrainCAConMark.png/440px-BrainCAConMark.png 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="954" data-file-width="974" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg/220px-Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg/330px-Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg/440px-Glioblastoma_-_MR_sagittal_with_contrast.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glioblastoma_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="600" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg/220px-Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg/330px-Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg/440px-Glioblastoma_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glioblastoma_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glioblastoma (histology slide)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When viewed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a>, glioblastomas often appear as ring-enhancing lesions. The appearance is not specific, however, as other lesions such as <a href="/wiki/Abscess" title="Abscess">abscess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tumefactive_multiple_sclerosis" title="Tumefactive multiple sclerosis">tumefactive multiple sclerosis</a>, and other entities may have a similar appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Definitive diagnosis of a suspected GBM on CT or MRI requires a <a href="/wiki/Stereotactic_biopsy" title="Stereotactic biopsy">stereotactic biopsy</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Craniotomy" title="Craniotomy">craniotomy</a> with tumor resection and pathologic confirmation. Because the tumor grade is based upon the most malignant portion of the tumor, biopsy or subtotal tumor resection can result in undergrading of the lesion. Imaging of tumor blood flow using perfusion MRI and measuring tumor metabolite concentration with <a href="/wiki/In_vivo_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="In vivo magnetic resonance spectroscopy">MR spectroscopy</a> may add value to standard MRI in select cases by showing increased relative cerebral blood volume and increased choline peak respectively, but pathology remains the gold standard for diagnosis and molecular characterization.</p>
<p>It is important to distinguish primary glioblastoma from secondary glioblastoma. These tumors occur spontaneously (<i>de novo</i>) or have progressed from a lower-grade glioma, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Primary glioblastomas have a worse prognosis, different tumor biology and may have a different response to therapy, which makes this a critical evaluation to determine patient prognosis and therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-35">[35]</a></sup> Over 80% of secondary glioblastoma carries a mutation in <i>IDH1</i>, whereas this mutation is rare in primary glioblastoma (5-10%). Thus, <i>IDH1</i> mutations are a useful tool to distinguish primary and secondary glioblastomas since histopathologically they are very similar and the distinction without molecular biomarkers is unreliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is very difficult to treat glioblastoma due to several complicating factors:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12672279_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12672279-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The tumor cells are very resistant to conventional therapies.</li>
<li>The brain is susceptible to damage due to conventional therapy.</li>
<li>The brain has a very limited capacity to repair itself.</li>
<li>Many drugs cannot cross the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a> to act on the tumor.</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment of primary brain tumors and brain metastases consists of both symptomatic and palliative therapies.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symptomatic_therapy">Symptomatic therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Symptomatic therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Supportive treatment focuses on relieving symptoms and improving the patient’s neurologic function. The primary supportive agents are <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li>Historically, around 90% of patients with glioblastoma underwent anticonvulsant treatment, although it has been estimated that only approximately 40% of patients required this treatment. Recently, it has been recommended that neurosurgeons not administer anticonvulsants prophylactically, and should wait until a seizure occurs before prescribing this medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Those receiving <a href="/wiki/Phenytoin" title="Phenytoin">phenytoin</a> concurrent with radiation may have serious skin reactions such as <a href="/wiki/Erythema_multiforme" title="Erythema multiforme">erythema multiforme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stevens%E2%80%93Johnson_syndrome" title="Stevens–Johnson syndrome">Stevens–Johnson syndrome</a>.</li>
<li>Corticosteroids, usually <a href="/wiki/Dexamethasone" title="Dexamethasone">dexamethasone</a> given 4 to 8 mg every 4 to 6 h, can reduce peritumoral edema (through rearrangement of the blood–brain barrier), diminishing mass effect and lowering intracranial pressure, with a decrease in headache or drowsiness.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Palliative_therapy">Palliative therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Palliative therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Palliative treatment usually is conducted to improve quality of life and to achieve a longer survival time. It includes surgery, radiation therapy, and chemotherapy. A maximally feasible resection with maximal tumor-free margins is usually performed along with external beam <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a>. Gross total resection of tumor is associated with a better prognosis.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Surgery is the first stage of treatment of glioblastoma. An average GBM tumor contains 10<sup>11</sup> cells, which is on average reduced to 10<sup>9</sup> cells after surgery (a reduction of 99%). Benefits of surgery include resection for a pathological diagnosis, alleviation of symptoms related to mass effect, and potentially removing disease before secondary resistance to radiotherapy and chemotherapy occurs.</p>
<p>The greater the extent of tumor removal, the better. Removal of 98% or more of the tumor has been associated with a significantly longer healthier time than if less than 98% of the tumor is removed in retrospective analyses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The chances of near-complete initial removal of the tumor may be increased if the surgery is guided by a fluorescent dye known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-aminolevulinic_acid" title="5-aminolevulinic acid">5-aminolevulinic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> GBM cells are widely infiltrative through the brain at diagnosis, and so despite a "total resection" of all obvious tumor, most people with GBM later develop recurrent tumors either near the original site or at more distant locations within the brain. Other modalities, typically radiation and chemotherapy, are used after surgery in an effort to suppress and slow recurrent disease.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiotherapy">Radiotherapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Radiotherapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Subsequent to surgery, radiotherapy becomes the mainstay of treatment for people with glioblastoma. It is typically performed along with giving temozolomide (TMZ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-RTG1-6">[6]</a></sup> A pivotal clinical trial carried out in the early 1970s showed that among 303 GBM patients randomized to radiation or nonradiation therapy, those who received radiation had a median survival more than double those who did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Subsequent clinical research has attempted to build on the backbone of surgery followed by radiation. On average, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a> after surgery can reduce the tumor size to 10<sup>7</sup> cells. Whole-brain radiotherapy does not improve when compared to the more precise and targeted three-dimensional conformal radiotherapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> A total radiation dose of 60–65 Gy has been found to be optimal for treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>GBM tumors are well known to contain zones of tissue exhibiting <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a> which are highly resistant to radiotherapy. Various approaches to chemotherapy radiosensitizers have been pursued with limited success as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> newer research approaches included preclinical and clinical investigations into the use of an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_diffusion-enhancing_compound" title="Oxygen diffusion-enhancing compound">oxygen diffusion-enhancing compound</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_sodium_crocetinate" title="Trans sodium crocetinate">trans sodium crocetinate</a> (TSC) as <a href="/wiki/Radiosensitizer" title="Radiosensitizer">radiosensitizers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> a clinical trial was underway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boron_neutron_capture_therapy" title="Boron neutron capture therapy">Boron neutron capture therapy</a> has been tested as an alternative treatment for glioblastoma multiforme but is not in common use.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemotherapy">Chemotherapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Chemotherapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most studies show no benefit from the addition of chemotherapy. However, a large clinical trial of 575 participants randomized to standard radiation versus radiation plus <a href="/wiki/Temozolomide" title="Temozolomide">temozolomide</a> chemotherapy showed that the group receiving temozolomide survived a median of 14.6 months as opposed to 12.1 months for the group receiving radiation alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG1_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-RTG1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> This treatment regime is now standard for most cases of glioblastoma where the person is not enrolled in a <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trial</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Temozolomide seems to work by sensitizing the tumor cells to radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>High doses of temozolomide in high-grade gliomas yield low toxicity, but the results are comparable to the standard doses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiangiogenic_therapy" title="Antiangiogenic therapy">Antiangiogenic therapy</a> with medications such as <a href="/wiki/Bevacizumab" title="Bevacizumab">bevacizumab</a> control symptoms but do not affect overall survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_modalities">Other modalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other modalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alternating_electric_field_therapy" title="Alternating electric field therapy">Alternating electric field therapy</a> is an FDA-approved therapy for newly diagnosed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and recurrent glioblastoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NovoTTF_FDA_approval_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NovoTTF_FDA_approval-63">[63]</a></sup> In 2015, initial results from a phase-three randomized clinical trial of alternating electric field therapy plus temozolomide in newly diagnosed glioblastoma reported a three-month improvement in progression-free survival, and a five-month improvement in overall survival compared to temozolomide therapy alone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stupp2015_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stupp2015-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sampson2015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sampson2015-65">[65]</a></sup> representing the first large trial in a decade to show a survival improvement in this setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sampson2015_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sampson2015-65">[65]</a></sup> Despite these results, the efficacy of this approach remains controversial among medical experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The median survival time from the time of diagnosis without any treatment is 3 months, but with treatment survival of 1–2 years is common. Increasing age (&gt; 60 years of age) carries a worse prognostic risk. Death is usually due to widespread tumor infiltration with <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_edema" title="Cerebral edema">cerebral edema</a> and increased <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_pressure" title="Intracranial pressure">intracranial pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi101093brainawm204_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi101093brainawm204-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>A good initial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karnofsky_score" title="Karnofsky score">Karnofsky Performance Score</a> (KPS) and <a href="/wiki/O-6-methylguanine-DNA_methyltransferase" title="O-6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase">MGMT</a> <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">methylation</a> are associated with longer survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi101093brainawm204_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi101093brainawm204-67">[67]</a></sup> A DNA test can be conducted on glioblastomas to determine whether or not the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)">promoter</a> of the <i>MGMT</i> <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylated" title="Methylated">methylated</a>. Patients with a methylated MGMT promoter have longer survival than those with an unmethylated MGMT promoter, due in part to increased sensitivity to temozolomide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This DNA characteristic is intrinsic to the patient and currently cannot be altered externally. Another positive prognostic marker for glioblastoma patients is mutation of the <i><a href="/wiki/IDH1" title="IDH1">IDH1</a></i> gene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ble2012_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ble2012-1">[1]</a></sup> which can be tested by DNA-based methods or by immunohistochemistry using an antibody against the most common mutation, namely IDH1-R132H.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-P2-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>More prognostic power can be obtained by combining the mutational status of <i>IDH1</i> and the methylation status of <i>MGMT</i> into a two-gene predictor. Patients with both <i>IDH1</i> mutations and <i>MGMT</i> methylation have the longest survival, patients with an <i>IDH1</i> mutation or <i>MGMT</i> methylation an intermediate survival and patients without either genetic event have the shortest survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Long-term benefits have also been associated with those patients who receive surgery, radiotherapy, and temozolomide chemotherapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi101093brainawm204_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-doi101093brainawm204-67">[67]</a></sup> However, much remains unknown about why some patients survive longer with glioblastoma. Age of under 50 is linked to longer survival in glioblastoma multiforme, as is 98%+ resection and use of temozolomide chemotherapy and better Karnofsky performance scores. A recent study confirms that younger age is associated with a much better prognosis, with a small fraction of patients under 40 years of age achieving a population-based cure. The population-based cure is thought to occur when a population's risk of death returns to that of the normal population, and in GBM, this is thought to occur after 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>UCLA Neuro-Oncology publishes real-time survival data for patients with this diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> They are the only institution in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> that shows how their patients are performing. They also show a listing of chemotherapy agents used to treat GBM tumors. Despite a poor prognosis, there is a small number of survivors who have been GBM free for more than 10–20 years.</p>
<p>According to a 2003 study, glioblastoma multiforme prognosis can be divided into three subgroups dependent on KPS, the age of the patient, and treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>About 3 per 100,000 people develop the disease a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> It most often begins around 64 years of age and occurs more commonly in males than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCR2014.2C5.16_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-WCR2014.2C5.16-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2015_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2015-4">[4]</a></sup> It is the second most common <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> cancer after <a href="/wiki/Meningioma" title="Meningioma">meningiomas</a>, which arise from the <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mc2016_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mc2016-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term glioblastoma multiforme was introduced in 1926 by <a href="/wiki/Percival_Bailey" title="Percival Bailey">Percival Bailey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Cushing" title="Harvey Cushing">Harvey Cushing</a>, based on the idea that the tumor originates from primitive precursors of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glial_cell" title="Glial cell">glial cells</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glioblast" title="Glioblast">glioblasts</a>), and the highly variable appearance due to the presence of necrosis, hemorrhage and cysts (multiform).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A 2014 investigation made a screening of various drugs for anti-glioblastoma activity and identified 22 drugs with potent anti-glioblastoma activity, including the combination of <a href="/wiki/Irinotecan" title="Irinotecan">irinotecan</a> and statins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid_.3D_24433351_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid_.3D_24433351-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Laboratory research using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_engineered" title="Genetically engineered">genetically engineered</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">stem cells</a> to target glioblastomas in mice was reported in 2014 to show promise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="MicroRNA">MicroRNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: MicroRNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>RNA interference, usually microRNA, is being studied in tissue culture, pathology specimens and in preclinical animal studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> MicroRNA-screening of <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a> is used to determine the prognosis of glioblastoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Immunotherapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Relapse of glioblastoma is attributed to the recurrence and persistence of tumor stem cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In a small trial, a tumor B-cell hybridoma vaccine against tumor stem cells elicited a specific tumor immune reaction thus enhancing immune response to the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Larger trials, including tests of different EGFR signaling patterns and their relationship to tumor stem cells are being conducted.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The test of <a href="/wiki/Rindopepimut" title="Rindopepimut">rindopepimut</a> failed in a phase III trial in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Other immunotherapeutic and vaccine-type approaches are at different stages of development, but conclusive results are not yet available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_therapy">Gene therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Gene therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">Gene therapy</a> is a promising approach for fighting cancers including brain cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Unlike current conventional cancer treatments such as chemotherapy and radiation therapy, gene transfer has the potential to selectively kill cancer cells while leaving healthy cells unharmed. Over the past two decades significant advances have been made in gene transfer technology and the field has matured to the point of clinical and commercial feasibility. Advances include vector (gene delivery vehicle) construction, vector producer cell efficiency and scale-up processes, preclinical models for target diseases and regulatory guidance regarding clinical trial design including endpoint definitions and measurements.</p>
<p>In one such approach, researchers at UCLA in 2005 reported a long-term survival benefit in an experimental brain tumor animal model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Subsequently, in preparation for human clinical trials, this technology was further developed by Tocagen, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toca_511" title="Toca 511">Toca 511</a> is since 2010 under clinical investigation in a Phase I trial for the potential treatment of recurrent high grade glioma including glioblastoma multiforme (GBM) and anaplastic astrocytoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCT01156584_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCT01156584-87">[87]</a></sup> Study due to complete July 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCT01156584_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCT01156584-87">[87]</a></sup> As of January 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Six different trials of Toca 511 are registered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intranasal_drug_delivery">Intranasal drug delivery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Intranasal drug delivery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Direct nose-to-brain drug delivery is being explored as a means to achieve higher, and hopefully more effective, drug concentrations in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> A clinical phase I/II study with glioblastoma patients in Brazil investigated the natural compound <a href="/wiki/Perillyl_alcohol" title="Perillyl alcohol">perillyl alcohol</a> for intranasal delivery as an <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a>. The results were encouraging<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and as of 2016 a similar trial has been initiated in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glioblastoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
